  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_5dfa690d-e6e8-4a48-8733-67e316251866'
      });
    }

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13444015759876028118">Maggie O’Farrell takes readers on a journey to the darker places of the human heart, where desires struggle with the imposition of social mores. This haunting story explores the seedy past of Victorian asylums, the oppression of family secrets, and the way truth can change everything.<br/><br/>In the middle of tending to the everyday business at her vintage clothing shop and sideste</span>
  <span id="freeText13444015759876028118" style="display:none">Maggie O’Farrell takes readers on a journey to the darker places of the human heart, where desires struggle with the imposition of social mores. This haunting story explores the seedy past of Victorian asylums, the oppression of family secrets, and the way truth can change everything.<br /><br />In the middle of tending to the everyday business at her vintage clothing shop and sidestepping her married boyfriend’s attempts at commitment, Iris Lockhart receives a stunning phone call: Her great-aunt Esme, whom she never knew existed, is being released from Cauldstone Hospital - where she has been locked away for over sixty years. Iris’s grandmother Kitty always claimed to be an only child. But Esme’s papers prove she is Kitty’s sister, and Iris can see the shadow of her dead father in Esme’s face. Esme has been labeled harmless - sane enough to coexist with the rest of the world. But Esme’s still basically a stranger, a family member never mentioned by the family, and one who is sure to bring life-altering secrets with her when she leaves the ward. If Iris takes her in, what dangerous truths might she inherit? <br /><br />Maggie O’Farrell’s intricate tale of family secrets, lost lives, and the freedom brought by truth will haunt readers long past its final page.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/167918-this-book-had-several-grey-areas-which-i">This book had several grey areas which I was a little confused about. Was Esme literally disappearing or just wishing to disappear?

I thought it was just metaphorically, but in the story she practices her meditating that helps her &quot;disappear.&quot; Also she shows up in photos as a blur.

Also what was with that spectre in the garden or whatever? I was so confused who/what he was</a>
</div>

<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/30254767-deborah-allin"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1512970959i/30254767._UY100_CR16,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>

<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/30254767-deborah-allin">Deborah Allin</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Dissociation is a huge part of being traumatised. If you have carried a secret or had to hide a truth for fear of the impact, it was as if you had to <a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Dissociation is a huge part of being traumatised. If you have carried a secret or had to hide a truth for fear of the impact, it was as if you had to disappear or disconnect so on some level she disappears because it is the only way she can survive in a world which does not allow her to exist. Also a thought. Could the spectre in the garden be the lost child or someone else associated to her, or the ghost of her painful experience.. just some thoughts. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1270376-any-thoughts-as-to-why-o-farrell-included">Any thoughts as to why O&#39;Farrell included the - almost but not really - incest story between Alex and Iris? Was the Author saying - all families have secrets don&#39;t judge (or incarcerate)? Or (when Kitty caught them at it) Iris IS the bloodline of Esme because just look at how she conducts herself?
Any ideas greatly received.</a>
</div>

<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/50015675-polly-mclean"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1449867386i/50015675._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/50015675-polly-mclean">Polly McLean</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">I thought that it was more to highlight the difference time has made to the way women's behaviour is judged. Iris's nonconformity to social norms isn'<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">I thought that it was more to highlight the difference time has made to the way women's behaviour is judged. Iris's nonconformity to social norms isn't considered especially odd and doesn't have any negative impact on her her life or well-being, while Esme's lesser nonconformity causes her to be deprived of her freedom for 61 years. <a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>

<div id="review_202485127" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/202485127" />
    <a title="Laura" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1394928-laura"><img alt="Laura" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1405367803p2/1394928.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/202485127?book_show_action=true">Aug 26, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laura" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laura" href="/user/show/1394928-laura">Laura</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780151014118" href="/book/show/228836.The_Vanishing_Act_of_Esme_Lennox">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People looking for a quick read with twisted family dynamics</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=hf">hf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=13-wishes">13-wishes</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=library">library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=ashley-s-list">ashley-s-list</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=a-lucky-one">a-lucky-one</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=download-library">download-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1394928-laura?shelf=fis-list">fis-list</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer202485127" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17603727882105049923">Man, I love to read. Opening a book by an author you've never read is like having a plane ticket to an unknown destination. You don't know where you're going, or exactly when you'll arrive. You just have to trust the pilot to get you there in one piece, hopefully with a smile on your face. Maggie O'Farrell doesn't disappoint, let me just tell you.<br/><br/>I don't think I'd have ever added this book if I paid lots of attention to the title or the cover. Both make me think of a frilly-froo-froo type read a</span>
  <span id="freeText17603727882105049923" style="display:none">Man, I love to read. Opening a book by an author you've never read is like having a plane ticket to an unknown destination. You don't know where you're going, or exactly when you'll arrive. You just have to trust the pilot to get you there in one piece, hopefully with a smile on your face. Maggie O'Farrell doesn't disappoint, let me just tell you.<br /><br />I don't think I'd have ever added this book if I paid lots of attention to the title or the cover. Both make me think of a frilly-froo-froo type read about women who love to shop and/or care about fashion. Which is pretty much the UN-me. Quoting now from page one, so this is no spoiler:<br /><br /><blockquote>
  <i>Let us begin with two girls at a dance. ... Or perhaps not. Perhaps it begins earlier, before the party, before they dressed in their new finery, before the candles were lit, before the sand was sprinkled on the boards, before the year whose end they are celebrating began. Who knows? Either way it ends at a grille covering a window with each square exactly two thumbnails wide.</i>
</blockquote><br /><br />I hope the author or publisher reads this review. For the love of God, please, <i>please</i> release another edition of this book with a new cover: a close-up of that window with an eye pressed to it, peering out. Just an eye. In the corner. And watch the sales skyrocket. The cover of the book I read, which is a girl looking down at her fancy blue dress barely tiptoes around the fringes of what the book is about. You want to draw a reader who will <i>appreciate</i> this book? Change the cover, and boom. <br /><br />In this book, you will float through the minds of several people. Two of them can be quite disjointed in their thoughts. She puts you right there. You will experience everything. This isn't exactly an easy read. It's a fast read, but you can't help but cringe at times as the horribleness begins to unfold. Still, it's hard to put down. You'll skip from past to present, and from one character to the next, without any chapter breaks at all. She simply skips a few lines and presents you with another scene. Loved the audio performance too, by Anne Flosnik.<br /><br />Highly recommended. Let me know what you think of this one. This would be a great book for a group discussion.<br /><br />ETA: Be careful you don't read too many reviews prior to the book. The less you know, the better. I really just wanted to try and convince people to look beyond the horrid cover and title. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17603727882105049923" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1629619763" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1629619763" />
    <a title="Debbie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8665845-debbie"><img alt="Debbie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1334166735p2/8665845.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1629619763?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Debbie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Debbie" href="/user/show/8665845-debbie">Debbie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=all-time-favorites">all-time-favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=secret-gems">secret-gems</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=mostly-not-set-in-u-s">mostly-not-set-in-u-s</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=luscious-spines-live-in-my-bookcase">luscious-spines-live-in-my-bookcase</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=cultural">cultural</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=complicated-female-relationships">complicated-female-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=bookcase-of-faves-at-home">bookcase-of-faves-at-home</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=loony-bins">loony-bins</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8665845-debbie?shelf=cool-chicks">cool-chicks</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1629619763" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3044802044494825968"><b>Hand me the WD40!</b><br/><br/>I know you all are probably getting sick of my pogo stick, but damn, here I go again, hopping like crazy! Can someone lend me some WD40? The damn thing is getting squeaky from overuse! But I’m not complaining. <br/><br/><b>I love loony bins!</b><br/>This book, oh, this book. The story is about Esme, a woman who has spent 60 years in a loony bin and suddenly is released into the custody of her grand-niece, whom she has never met. (Juicy already, right?) So 60 years?!! Come on, right there the book had</span>
  <span id="freeText3044802044494825968" style="display:none"><b>Hand me the WD40!</b><br /><br />I know you all are probably getting sick of my pogo stick, but damn, here I go again, hopping like crazy! Can someone lend me some WD40? The damn thing is getting squeaky from overuse! But I’m not complaining. <br /><br /><b>I love loony bins!</b><br />This book, oh, this book. The story is about Esme, a woman who has spent 60 years in a loony bin and suddenly is released into the custody of her grand-niece, whom she has never met. (Juicy already, right?) So 60 years?!! Come on, right there the book had me salivating. I love psychology, and frankly, I love crazy characters. I immediately had a zillion questions. How crazy is she, and what does it look like? What if she isn’t crazy at all? Did someone commit her and throw away the key, or did she claim her spot as a crackpot and hang out a sign, Home Sweet Home? Why was she there so eff-ing long? What’s it like inside the joint? Plenty of juicy questions, which could only have juicy answers. I was ready to scarf down this slurpie! <br /><br /><b>It’s jumpy all right…</b><br />There are three narrators: Esme, her sister, and her grand-niece. Each of them chirps up unexpectedly, sometimes in the middle of a paragraph even, squeezing out the last character who was just getting started. Yeah, it’s jumpy all right—there are even two time periods. Who knew whether this would work for me. Would I get totally confused and go running to my Complaint Board? No way, not with a master like O’Farrell. I realized early on that my pogo stick had a mind of its own, leading me from person to person, past to present, ecstatically cutting through time and space with ease.<br /><br /><b>Trying to sell the jumpy.</b><br />I was trying to sell this jumpy style to a friend who lives by linear. He was not impressed. Super annoyed even by the idea of it, he wanted to know why the author didn’t just tell the story with a normal beginning, middle, and end, like real life. So I had to wrack my brain to figure it out myself. I realized this is what was so cool: Each of the three stories was completely spell-binding. I’d leave one exciting story and be immediately dropped into another story that was just as exciting. The drama is non-stop, yet it’s never over-the-top or gratuitous. There’s tension out the ying-yang. So how does O’Farrell manage to make everything seem like a cliffhanger? This isn’t a thriller, so how does that work? Answer: She’s such an amazing writer, she makes you feel like every single scene is intense and important. She makes sure you have no choice but to read on, to glue your eyes to the page. You gobble up the secrets as you try in vain to figure out where the story is going. The ending is a whopper.<br /><br /><b>Like me best!</b><br />Each story was saying, “Like me the best!” “I do, I do!”, I yelled back. I wasn’t lying. I like each one the best. One story would grab my attention and hold on to it tight, and I was mesmerized, hypnotized, and immersed in its sea of drama. And then in the very next paragraph, I’d be in another world and on full alert again. It felt smooth and oddly peaceful, this floating on top of her words, peeking into the souls of these intriguing characters. <br /><br /><b>I hear a symphony</b><br />The story is so creative, the writing so good. The fact that O’Farrell could come up with such an amazing storyline and could intertwine the three voices so artfully just left me in awe. It’s like a symphony where there are a bunch of solos, all different instruments, all equally rich and unique, and then they play together and it’s dynamite. <br /><br /><b>A feisty kiddo</b><br />As a kid, Esme is quirky, feisty, independent. She’s engaged in the world with such zest and intensity it’s hard not to love her. It’s her misfortune to be raised in 1930s Scotland, where life is full of rules. Esme doesn’t fit the mold of what a girl should act like, and this can get her in trouble.<br /><br /><b>60 years later…</b><br />As an adult, Esme is an enigma. What’s she going to be like now, as she is suddenly being collected up from the loony bin by her grand-niece, Iris, whom she has never met? Is Esme dangerous? How will she react to the free world? <br /><br /><b>Just imagine yourself in her shoes</b><br />Iris’s story grabbed me, too. She’s so earnest, curious, wary, and overwhelmed. And she has her own secrets. But never mind her various interesting feelings—just imagine having a stranger who has spent 60 years in a loony bin come stay with you! That, right there. THAT! That’s what has me bowing to O’Farrell. What an irresistible storyline she has created!<br /><br /><b>Go into it blind</b><br />I don’t want to give away any of the plot, which is sort of complicated but still very accessible. (I just love literary fiction that is accessible!) It’s great to go into this one blind. Just know that it’s a haunting story about siblings, secrets, guilt. We get to see the past through both Esme’s and her sister’s eyes, and we get to see the present through both Esme’s and her grand-niece’s eyes. I love it when we get to see what’s going on from two points of view.<br /><br /><b>Why this book?</b><br />I read this book because I was high on O’Farrell’s memoir, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35137915.I_Am__I_Am__I_Am_Seventeen_Brushes_with_Death" title="I Am, I Am, I Am Seventeen Brushes with Death by Maggie O'Farrell" rel="nofollow noopener">I Am, I Am, I Am: Seventeen Brushes with Death</a>. I knew her language fits me—it makes me feel all floaty and it makes my soul all happy. And as I said, I love crazy and I love loony bins—as any zealous ex-Psych major would. <br /><br /><b>Hopping!</b><br />Beauteous language. Complex, juicy plot. Intriguing characters. Surprises. This book delivers all that, and it swept me away into another world. I couldn’t put it down. Yes, it’s pogo-stick time, as I hop on over to my all-time favorites pile and gleefully toss this book onto it!</span>
  <a data-text-id="3044802044494825968" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1036127480" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1036127480" />
    <a title="Angela M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m"><img alt="Angela M" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1512600770p2/4685500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1036127480?book_show_action=true">Aug 25, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Angela M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Angela M" href="/user/show/4685500-angela-m">Angela M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1036127480" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3169440480213307899">The opening of this novel reflects the simple beauty and power of O'Farrell's writing and I was immediately drawn into this story.<br/><br/>“Let us begin with two girls at a dance.<br/> They are at the edge of the room. One sits on a chair, opening and shutting a dance-card with gloved fingers. The other stands beside, watching the dance unfold: the circling couples, the clasped hands, the drumming shoes, the whirling skirts, the bounce of the floor. It is the last hour of the year and the windows behind them </span>
  <span id="freeText3169440480213307899" style="display:none">The opening of this novel reflects the simple beauty and power of O'Farrell's writing and I was immediately drawn into this story.<br /><br />“Let us begin with two girls at a dance.<br /> They are at the edge of the room. One sits on a chair, opening and shutting a dance-card with gloved fingers. The other stands beside, watching the dance unfold: the circling couples, the clasped hands, the drumming shoes, the whirling skirts, the bounce of the floor. It is the last hour of the year and the windows behind them are blank with night. The seated girl is dressed in something pale, Esme forgets what, the other in a dark red frock that doesn’t suit her. She has lost her gloves. It begins here.<br /> Or perhaps not.”<br /><br />It was not long before my heart was broken for Esme Lennox, a precocious , inquisitive, sometimes misbehaving little girl who is not what her parents want her to be. She suffers the cruelty of her mother and father in some scenes that are just so difficult to read. Esme is tied to a chair so she doesn't crawl under the table during dinner. Everyone jumps up and leaves the table ( it wasn’t clear to me<br /> why ) but they forget about her. The family goes on a trip and leaves Esme , as they don’t want to deal with her, home with the nursemaid and her baby brother Hugo. While they are gone, the unthinkable happens , and Esme is alone for several days and traumatized by what has happened. The cruelty continues and Esme mother won’t speak to her or even look at her.<br /><br />There are three narratives in the story : flashbacks of Esme from her childhood days in India and then in Scotland, and from her sister Kitty from those same times and places. The third is the present day story of Iris, Kitty’s granddaughter. These move swiftly from past to present to past again without any warning. It was at first a little confusing but then it becomes clear as the book moves on who is thinking or speaking.<br /><br />If you’ve read the description of the book, you know that Esme has been in a mental institution for 60 years. This reminded me so much of a book I recently read , "What She Left Behind" also about a woman committed because her father said so , not because she was insane.”<br /><br /><br />Before the end I was able to figure out something about Esme that is not revealed until near the end but the book was no less captivating . As for the end - I woke up this morning thinking about it. I was not expecting it. This is a moving story that won’t leave me anytime soon.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3169440480213307899" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <a title="PorshaJo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="PorshaJo" href="/user/show/5464046-porshajo">PorshaJo</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=challengereads">challengereads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5464046-porshajo?shelf=audio">audio</a>,

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2180917424" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12965066032919841269">Rating 4.5<br/><br/>I kept seeing all these wonderful review for books by Maggie O'Farrell. I kept saying, oh I need to read her, that's one author I've yet to try, and I added her to my TBR pile.....where she remained for some time. Recently read another review by my GR friend Angela and I said now I must finally read one of her books. So I grabbed the audio version of this book and I must say....I'm hooked. I want to read all her books.<br/><br/>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox is....is....hauntingly beautiful, f</span>
  <span id="freeText12965066032919841269" style="display:none">Rating 4.5<br /><br />I kept seeing all these wonderful review for books by Maggie O'Farrell. I kept saying, oh I need to read her, that's one author I've yet to try, and I added her to my TBR pile.....where she remained for some time. Recently read another review by my GR friend Angela and I said now I must finally read one of her books. So I grabbed the audio version of this book and I must say....I'm hooked. I want to read all her books.<br /><br />The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox is....is....hauntingly beautiful, frightfully scary, wildly inventive. I'm not sure how to describe it. You get a glimpse of asylums of long ago, injustice, jealousy, family secrets, revenge, of being different, or just conforming to others ideas, and so much more. Esme Lennox has been in Cauldstone Hospital for sixty years but due to the hospital closing, she is being released. She is released to a family member listed as a contact, her niece, Iris. Iris's grandmother, Kitty, is in a home and has alzheimers, but she has always indicated that she was an only child. So the three of their 'stories' converge in a spectacular, heartbreaking way. The story slowly unfolds by rotating between Esme, Iris, and Kitty's view point.<br /><br />I'm so glad I finally read this one and I can't wait to read more of O'Farrell's books. I've already ordered a few more. Now, the audio. This was a problem for me. The narrator has a wonderful voice and I loved her accent. I can still hear her voice. But the complex nature of the story, I think, did not lend well to audio. I grabbed the print and was reading after listening or switching back and forth. The book - it has no chapters, it switches quickly between the three points of views, changing so quickly. Audio required a lot of concentration and I truly felt that I'm missing this beautiful story and grabbed the print. Even now that I've finished the book, I still keep going back to the print. Overall, a wonderful read, one that I feel will haunt my dreams for some time.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12965066032919841269" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2752994-dem?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer504311317" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6259179089325843655">The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox is a beautifully written and haunting story about a woman who has been unjustly incarcerated in a mental hospital at a very young age and has remained there for over sixty years. The hospital is now closing down and the inhabitants have to be rehoused. The story is set between the 1930s and the present day.<br/><br/>The story is intelligently told and the plot is really well-thought out so there were enough twists and turns to keep me engrossed and intrigued. The way Maggi</span>
  <span id="freeText6259179089325843655" style="display:none">The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox is a beautifully written and haunting story about a woman who has been unjustly incarcerated in a mental hospital at a very young age and has remained there for over sixty years. The hospital is now closing down and the inhabitants have to be rehoused. The story is set between the 1930s and the present day.<br /><br />The story is intelligently told and the plot is really well-thought out so there were enough twists and turns to keep me engrossed and intrigued. The way Maggie O Farrell writes is just exquisite, so beautifully descriptive that I was carried away and felt totally drawn into the story and life of Esme Lennox. The character development is perfect and sometimes in a book you come across a character that you completely fall in love with and I fell in love with the character of Esme.<br /><br />This is a short novel but the author does not waste a single word, she really is able to convey so many emotions in each chapter.<br /><br />I love haunting and intriguing novels and The vanishing Acts of Esme Lennox was exactly what I love.<br /><br />I did find myself having to re-read certain paragraphs in this book especially the narrative of Kitty as this is one of those books the you need to pay attention while reading or you may get a little lost.<br /><br /><b> Re-read this book for a book club read and I enjoyed this book all over again, I really enjoyed the characters of Esme and Kittly and loved the parallels drawn between Iris and Esme's life. Look forward to the discussion on this one.</b></span>

          </span>

<div id="review_2484008844" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2484008844" />
    <a title="Esil" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3643764-esil"><img alt="Esil" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1443746908p2/3643764.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2484008844?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Esil" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Esil" href="/user/show/3643764-esil">Esil</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2484008844" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12911013229434909349">The only other book I have read by Maggie O’Farrell is <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35137915.I_Am__I_Am__I_Am_Seventeen_Brushes_with_Death' title='I Am, I Am, I Am Seventeen Brushes with Death by Maggie O'Farrell' rel='nofollow noopener'>I Am, I Am, I Am: Seventeen Brushes with Death</a>, which I loved. Having now read The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox, I see that O’Farrell has incredible talent. Her writing is phenomenal and her perspective on people, relationships and life is scary insightful. Iris finds out that she has a great aunt — Esme — she never knew existed who has been living in a psychiatric hospital for 60 years. Iris takes her great aunt home as the hospital is clos</span>
  <span id="freeText12911013229434909349" style="display:none">The only other book I have read by Maggie O’Farrell is <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/35137915.I_Am__I_Am__I_Am_Seventeen_Brushes_with_Death" title="I Am, I Am, I Am Seventeen Brushes with Death by Maggie O'Farrell" rel="nofollow noopener">I Am, I Am, I Am: Seventeen Brushes with Death</a>, which I loved. Having now read The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox, I see that O’Farrell has incredible talent. Her writing is phenomenal and her perspective on people, relationships and life is scary insightful. Iris finds out that she has a great aunt — Esme — she never knew existed who has been living in a psychiatric hospital for 60 years. Iris takes her great aunt home as the hospital is closing. The story is told from the perspectives of Iris, Esme and Kitty. Kitty is Iris’ grandmother and Esme’s sister, and she is suffering from Altzheimers. It turns out that there’s a heartbreaking backstory to Esme’s hospitalization. She is odd but certainly not mentally ill. Esme’s story comes out slowly through snippets of consciousness and memory from both Esme and Kitty. They spent their early childhood in India, and then moved to Edinburgh at a time when social norms were painfully rigid and there was no language available to discuss many topics. In such a world, Esme’s oddness makes her horribly vulnerable. And the ending is quite something. Beautiful, infuriating and heartbreaking. This book makes me want to read all of O’Farrell’s books. Thanks to Debbie for putting this one on my radar.</span>
  <a data-text-id="12911013229434909349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2787688523" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6283438130382169993">As a young girl, Esme Lennox is taken kicking and screaming from the family home and committed to an asylum known as Cauldstone Hospital.  61 years later, the hospital is shutting down and the now elderly woman is released.  Esme was certainly an irregular weave as a child, prone to outrageous behavior that flew in the face of decorum, a continuing source of embarrassment for her sister and her parents.  But what on earth must she have done to warrant obliteration from her family?  Was she truly</span>
  <span id="freeText6283438130382169993" style="display:none">As a young girl, Esme Lennox is taken kicking and screaming from the family home and committed to an asylum known as Cauldstone Hospital.  61 years later, the hospital is shutting down and the now elderly woman is released.  Esme was certainly an irregular weave as a child, prone to outrageous behavior that flew in the face of decorum, a continuing source of embarrassment for her sister and her parents.  But what on earth must she have done to warrant obliteration from her family?  Was she truly insane, or a victim of a grave injustice?  The idea of how a person can be effectively erased in this way is chilling.  <br /><br />I am having trouble striking the tone I want with this review, but suffice it to say the story is unlike any I have read, original and haunting.</span>

<div id="review_2484473024" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2484473024" />
    <a title="Cheri" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/106692-cheri"><img alt="Cheri" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1601402708p2/106692.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2484473024?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Cheri" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Cheri" href="/user/show/106692-cheri">Cheri</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/38469895-the-vanishing-act-of-esme-lennox">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=library-book">library-book</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=alzheimers-dementia">alzheimers-dementia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=family-relationships">family-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=india">india</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=scotland">scotland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/106692-cheri?shelf=irish-author">irish-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2484473024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1435142673595944212"><br/>I have been wanting to read Maggie O’Farrell’s novels for the last couple of years, ever since I first read my friend Angela’s review of <i>
  <b>This Must Be the Place</b>
</i> and I’d long planned to read some of these books that I’d had on my list to-read for too long, and so I’d scheduled a period of time to devote just to those books. There are so many I doubt I will get to them all, but I began this by reading O’Farrell’s<i>
  <b>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</b>
</i> - not because I’d chosen to read this one firs</span>
  <span id="freeText1435142673595944212" style="display:none"><br />I have been wanting to read Maggie O’Farrell’s novels for the last couple of years, ever since I first read my friend Angela’s review of <i>
  <b>This Must Be the Place</b>
</i> and I’d long planned to read some of these books that I’d had on my list to-read for too long, and so I’d scheduled a period of time to devote just to those books. There are so many I doubt I will get to them all, but I began this by reading O’Farrell’s<i>
  <b>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</b>
</i> - not because I’d chosen to read this one first, but because it was available through my library. I’m so glad that I did, this is beautifully written, heartbreaking, tragic and unputdownable. <br /><br /><i>”Her body sways like a branch in the wind and her stockinged feet pass over the carpet very lightly. Her head is so full of the tune and the cool swish of silk that she doesn’t hear the people coming up the stairs, she doesn’t hear anything. She has no idea that in a minute or two the door will fly open and they will be standing there in the doorway, looking at her. She hears the music and she feels the dress. That is all. Her hands move about her like small birds.”</i><br /><br /><i>”And Esme sees what might be. She shuts her mouth, closes her throat, folds her hands over each other and she does the thing she has perfected. Her speciality. To absent yourself, to make yourself vanish.”</i><br /><br />This story is recounted through several viewpoints, through the stories of Iris, Esme, and Esme’s sister Kitty. Both Esme and Kitty are now elderly, and Kitty, grandmother of Iris, is in a care facility, an Alzheimer’s patient. Esme has just been released into the care of Iris, and Iris is still trying to piece together how this woman she never knew existed until days before has come to be her responsibility, to be in her life. <br /><br />While the story that unfolds is a relatively fast read, it does skip from one narrator to another without much to mark the difference except breaks from one paragraph to a new one, an extra line or two in space, and someone else is relaying their views – still, I was always aware of the change in narrator when it happened, so I didn’t find it to be confusing, and I felt it gave a broader view of the overall story.<br /><br /><i>”We are all, Esme decides, just vessels through which identities pass: we are lent features, gestures, habits, then we hand them on. Nothing is our own. We begin the world as anagrams of our antecedents.”</i><br /><br />Recommended<br /><br />Many thanks to Angela, whose reviews prompted me to read so many books! <br />Angela’s review for The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox: <br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1036127480">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br />Angela’s review for “This Must Be the Place”<br /><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1615846725">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a><br /><br /><br />Many thanks, also, to the Public Library system, and the many Librarians that manage, organize and keep it running, for the loan of this book!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1435142673595944212" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15338889910057812225">This is a lovely, lovely book and I am amazed I have not read it before. I did not know what I was missing! It's not an easy book to review because you do need to come to it with no preconceived ideas about the content. Enough to say that it involves a family, a lot of memories about the past and a rather good ending! The writing is just beautifully done especially the way the author moves between the memories of Kitty and Esme, dropping clues along the way so the reader can begin to understand </span>
  <span id="freeText15338889910057812225" style="display:none">This is a lovely, lovely book and I am amazed I have not read it before. I did not know what I was missing! It's not an easy book to review because you do need to come to it with no preconceived ideas about the content. Enough to say that it involves a family, a lot of memories about the past and a rather good ending! The writing is just beautifully done especially the way the author moves between the memories of Kitty and Esme, dropping clues along the way so the reader can begin to understand the actual truth of things. In many ways it is a mystery and as it unfolds the reader begins to understand Esme and grieve with her for what she has lost.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer3414597565606705082">Unforgettable book with a stunning ending that will haunt me for some time to come. This is an outstanding book and a tragic and disturbing story...believable because what was done to Esme may not have been a rare occurrence for women in that time. As a child and young woman, Esme was naïve yet spirited…an independent thinker, which confounded her family. It is no secret that Esme was locked away in an institution for 61 years. The story is told from three points of view to gradually reveal the </span>
  <span id="freeText3414597565606705082" style="display:none">Unforgettable book with a stunning ending that will haunt me for some time to come. This is an outstanding book and a tragic and disturbing story...believable because what was done to Esme may not have been a rare occurrence for women in that time. As a child and young woman, Esme was naïve yet spirited…an independent thinker, which confounded her family. It is no secret that Esme was locked away in an institution for 61 years. The story is told from three points of view to gradually reveal the mystery of Esme’s heartbreaking family history. Four stars only because those three points of view as well as the past and present time frames are sometimes confusing and hard to follow.<br /><br /></span>

<div id="review_3434933091" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3434933091" />
    <a title="Sara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5512657-sara"><img alt="Sara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1543780395p2/5512657.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3434933091?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sara" href="/user/show/5512657-sara">Sara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Kindle Edition " href="/book/show/38469895-the-vanishing-act-of-esme-lennox">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=2020-aty-challenge">2020-aty-challenge</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=pleasure-reading">pleasure-reading</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=borrowed-from-library">borrowed-from-library</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=women-writers">women-writers</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=contemporary-fiction">contemporary-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5512657-sara?shelf=family-dynamics">family-dynamics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3434933091" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5374359917418762796">Cauldstone, the mental asylum, is closing. All the patients must be returned to their families or placed elsewhere, so Iris Lockhart is contacted regarding one of those patients, her great-aunt, Esme Lennox, a person whose existence is wholly unknown to her. Esme’s sister, Kitty, is suffering from Alzheimer’s and Iris’s father is dead. This responsibility falls solely upon her shoulders.<br/><br/>What Maggie O’Farrell gives us is Esme’s story, which is a sad and infuriating one, and Iris’s story which has</span>
  <span id="freeText5374359917418762796" style="display:none">Cauldstone, the mental asylum, is closing. All the patients must be returned to their families or placed elsewhere, so Iris Lockhart is contacted regarding one of those patients, her great-aunt, Esme Lennox, a person whose existence is wholly unknown to her. Esme’s sister, Kitty, is suffering from Alzheimer’s and Iris’s father is dead. This responsibility falls solely upon her shoulders.<br /><br />What Maggie O’Farrell gives us is Esme’s story, which is a sad and infuriating one, and Iris’s story which has at least one sad element of its own. Neither of these women does exactly what people expect of them, and one of them has paid a price beyond belief for being independent and different. <br /><br /><i>She shuts her mouth, closes her throat, folds her hands over each other and she does the thing she has perfected. Her specialty. To absent yourself, to make yourself vanish. Ladies and gentlemen, behold. It is most important to keep yourself very still. Even breathing can remind them that you are there; so only very short, very shallow breaths. Just enough to stay alive. And no more.</i><br /><br />Imagine doing that for so long that it becomes an art. Imagine a situation in which you might require that of another human being. Imagine that human being is your daughter, your sister, even your patient. The idea tied my stomach in knots. I have not felt this level of wanting to smash into a cell and free someone since reading Sebastian Barry’s <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3419808.The_Secret_Scripture" title="The Secret Scripture by Sebastian Barry" rel="nofollow noopener">The Secret Scripture</a>.<br /><br />This is a story about family, about sisters, about love and hate and jealousy and ruin and ignorance and intolerance, and a parent who can calmly turn his back and walk away from the unthinkable.<br /><br /><i>They have all narrowed down to this black-haired girl sitting on the sand, who has no idea that her hands and her eyes and the tilt of her head and the fall of her hair belong to Esme’s mother. We are all, Esme decides, just vessels through which identities pass; we are lent features, gestures, habits, then we hand them on. Nothing is our own. We begin in the world as anagrams of our antecedents.</i><br /><br />That quotation struck me as so true and profound. I have been researching my family line lately and, as I read about the women and men who came before me, I have wished so much that there were photographs that I might search for traces of the familiar. Who knows what parts of those others exist in those of us still here...bits of themselves left behind forever.<br /><br />I am not surprised to have loved this book. I have read enough reviews on Goodreads to know that Maggie O’Farrell is destined to be an author I want to read over and over again. I am excited to have my first one behind me. I cannot imagine they get any better than this.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="5374359917418762796" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer1692817468" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14444043745361849595"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1468077624i/19664766._SX540_.jpg' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>Hannah Gordon, Frances Grey and Eleanor Bron begin Maggie O'Farrell's highly acclaimed novel set between the conventions of 1930's Edinburgh and the freedoms of today.<br/><br/><img src='https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394121025r/8810947.gif?13941210255377' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b00770rf'>http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b00770rf</a><br/><br/>Description: <i>In the middle of tending to the everyday business at her vintage-clothing shop and sidestepping her married boyfriend’s attempts at commitment, Iris Lockhart receives a stunning phone call: Her great-aunt Esme, whom she never knew existed, is being released from Cauldstone Hospit</i></span>
  <span id="freeText14444043745361849595" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1468077624i/19664766._SX540_.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg">Hannah Gordon, Frances Grey and Eleanor Bron begin Maggie O'Farrell's highly acclaimed novel set between the conventions of 1930's Edinburgh and the freedoms of today.<br><br><img src="https://s3.amazonaws.com/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1394121025r/8810947.gif?13941210255377" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b00770rf">http://www.bbc.co.uk/programmes/b00770rf</a><br><br>Description: <i>In the middle of tending to the everyday business at her vintage-clothing shop and sidestepping her married boyfriend’s attempts at commitment, Iris Lockhart receives a stunning phone call: Her great-aunt Esme, whom she never knew existed, is being released from Cauldstone Hospital—where she has been locked away for more than sixty-one years.<br><br>Iris’s grandmother Kitty always claimed to be an only child. But Esme’s papers prove she is Kitty’s sister, and Iris can see the shadow of her dead father in Esme’s face.<br><br>Esme has been labeled harmless—sane enough to coexist with the rest of the world. But she's still basically a stranger, a family member never mentioned by the family, and one who is sure to bring life-altering secrets with her when she leaves the ward. If Iris takes her in, what dangerous truths might she inherit?<br><br>A gothic, intricate tale of family secrets, lost lives, and the freedom brought by truth, The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox will haunt you long past its final page.</i><br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>Let us begin with two girls at a dance</b>: One sits on a chair, opening and shutting a dance-card with gloved fingers. The other stands beside her, watching the dance unfold: the circling couples, the bounce of the floor. It begins here'.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>You've Got a Visitor</b>: Esme Lennox has been confined to a psychiatric hospital for 60 years. Her great-niece Iris has only just learned of her existence. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>Daughters Who Just Don't Listen</b>: Why was Esme incarcerated in an asylum? Iris goes in search of some answers and discovers a shameful past. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>The Granddaughter of an Advocate</b>: As Esme gets her first taste of freedom for over 60 years, forgotten memories of her privileged past are reawakened. Meanwhile, Iris must find her a home. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>I Would Like to Go to the Sea</b>: Esme returns to an old family haunt - North Berwick<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>Like Sugar in Water</b>Poignant story of the shameful treatment of unconventional women in the 1930s and the lengths a woman might go to, for the thing she wants most.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>I'm Not Going to Get Married, to Anyone</b>: To her family's surprise, Esme has a highly desirable suitor. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>She Wouldn't Stop Screaming</b>: Esme's tragic fate is sealed amid the New Year celebrations in Edinburgh.<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>She Wouldn't Let Go of the Baby</b>: Secrets are revealed as Kitty and Esme's memories collide. <br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1348843114s/250729.jpg" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><b>Can We Visit Kitty Today?</b>: After 61 years, Esme knows the truth and confronts her sister, Kitty.<br><br>Rummaging through Jenners on Princes Street was the icing on the cake of this quirky, sometimes fun, sometimes worrying, compelling story of the pudding-bowl haircut refuting, hidden deemed mad, great-aunt Esme.</span>

<div id="review_2597066206" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2597066206" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/8051792-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1544530609p2/8051792.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2597066206?book_show_action=true">Nov 20, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/8051792-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8051792-lisa?shelf=2018-favorites">2018-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2597066206" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6423503058847751504">[4.5] This haunting novel leaves most of Esme’s life to the reader’s imagination. For me that made her story even more powerful and sad. And infuriating - I was so angry with her family. O’Farrell brilliantly meshes the narratives of Iris, Kitty and Esme in a way that keeps illuminating and moving the novel forward. And the ending is quite fascinating...and hopeful too. I love that Iris doesn't let go. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_101425389" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101425389" />
    <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days"><img alt="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1420332941p2/395599.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101425389?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shannon (Giraffe Days)" href="/user/show/395599-shannon-giraffe-days">Shannon (Giraffe Days)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/395599-shannon-giraffe-days?shelf=2010">2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101425389" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12850608324045300993">Every now and then you come across a book so perfectly <i>whole</i>, so <i>complete</i> in itself, that you marvel as you read. It has such flow, such control of style, such effortless prose, that it's almost impossible to put it down. Such a book is <i>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</i>, which I could have easily read in one sitting except I had to go to work.<br/><br/><i>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</i> is the story of three women and the burning secrets that affected them all. Iris Lockhart is a young, single, modern women </span>
  <span id="freeText12850608324045300993" style="display:none">Every now and then you come across a book so perfectly <i>whole</i>, so <i>complete</i> in itself, that you marvel as you read. It has such flow, such control of style, such effortless prose, that it's almost impossible to put it down. Such a book is <i>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</i>, which I could have easily read in one sitting except I had to go to work.<br /><br /><i>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</i> is the story of three women and the burning secrets that affected them all. Iris Lockhart is a young, single, modern women living in Edinburgh, having an affair with a married man and pretending to herself that she didn't once have a secret relationship with her step-brother, Alex. But it's her life, and when she gets a call about a great-aunt locked up in a mental institute - an aunt she never knew existed - she wants none of the responsibility. Apart from her grandmother, Kitty, who has Alzheimer's, Iris is the only family member left.<br /><br />The great-aunt, Esme Lennox, is Kitty's younger sister. She's been locked away in Cauldstone for the last sixty years - since she was sixteen. Why? That is Esme's story to tell. But she was locked away, and vanished, and forgotten. Now she enters Iris' life, and what was started all those years ago must find an ending, a resolution. <br /><br />Esme's story is gripping. Esme the girl comes across strongly, vividly, an immediately familiar presence. An odd child, she grew into a modern teen who eschewed marriage and wanted to go to university. She didn't abide by the family's class-conscious values and was always going out without a hat on, or would forget her gloves. These weren't her only crimes. There's also a boy, and where there's a boy, there's trouble. <br /><br />The novel revolves around Esme's past, from her childhood in India to her school life in Edinburgh, and her early experiences in the asylum. Her story is complemented by Iris's messy life and Kitty's meandering thoughts as she strays randomly through the paths of her memories. Like an intricate tapestry, the scenes from the past weave together to make a whole, a powerful, moving story that's simple, cruel and tragic.<br /><br />What makes this story so beautiful and flow so well, is the prose. Told mostly in present tense, it shifts to past effortlessly, usually without me even noticing. That's actually hard to do - shift seamlessly, that is! I did have to read the first two pages twice, to get into the flow of how it was written, but after that it was like being picked up on a breath of memory and carried along, weightless, but ever observant. There are no chapter breaks, only section breaks to separate voice, and this adds to that feeling of flow, that great momentum that the story has. From the first page, you need to know what happens, and what happened. <br /><br />It is fitting to use present tense, to create a sense of timelessness, a sense of every memory having relevance. Kitty's memories don't stick out and jar, told as they are in Kitty's confused, muddled voice - confused but clear; that kind of paradox that's hard to describe but can be created nonetheless. Her voice is distinct, different from Esme's troubled mind. Again the use of present tense works to allow their memories to merge, to show how lost they are in these memories. It's like music, a song being played, the instruments breath and memory and loss and hope. <br /><br />It's horrible to think how easy it was to lock up your wayward daughter or reluctant (or "over-sexed") wife or troublesome sister. Esme wasn't an exceptional case. And this carried on well into the twentieth century. It is just one kind of crime against women. You'd like to think that it couldn't happen anymore but it does, in various forms.<br /><br />Some people found that there were too many loose ends, and the ending was too vague and open. I didn't find that there were any loose ends. Everything came together satisfactorily, and without any padding (it's a pretty short book, especially with how fast you can read it). And the ending, the ending was so right for the story. Yes it is somewhat open, but enough is shown to see where it will probably go, for good or worse.<br /><br />I wanted to share some quotes, as examples of prose. As usual, I didn't mark any exceptional quotes - this time because I was so caught up in the story it was hard to stop reading. I would share some drawn randomly, but I find that without context the magic falls away and the words become just ... words. Interesting how that happens.<br /><br />As a last note, my thanks to those who recommended this book. You were right!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12850608324045300993" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Carmel Hanes" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44095345-carmel-hanes"><img alt="Carmel Hanes" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1520284777p2/44095345.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3496085497?book_show_action=true">Sep 25, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Carmel Hanes" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Carmel Hanes" href="/user/show/44095345-carmel-hanes">Carmel Hanes</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44095345-carmel-hanes?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3496085497" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4124322978599577851">How can you love a book that you also want to throw against the wall? <br/><br/>You can when it captures heartbreaking injustice with withering clarity and style, speaks with unrelenting truth, and offers characters that are both ordinarily small and bigger than life. You can when it makes you care about a woman who's spent most of her life in an institution and the unspeakable reasons she was taken there and forced to remain. It can when the eloquent writing makes you want to jump into the pages to hold </span>
  <span id="freeText4124322978599577851" style="display:none">How can you love a book that you also want to throw against the wall? <br /><br />You can when it captures heartbreaking injustice with withering clarity and style, speaks with unrelenting truth, and offers characters that are both ordinarily small and bigger than life. You can when it makes you care about a woman who's spent most of her life in an institution and the unspeakable reasons she was taken there and forced to remain. It can when the eloquent writing makes you want to jump into the pages to hold her hand and not let go. It can when you know that, even though this is a fiction story, this kind of thing happened(s) to people.<br /><br />The novel offers three perspectives: Esme in both present time and remembering the past; her sister, Kitty, whose perspective is from the past; and Iris, who is the granddaughter/great niece who is meeting Esme for the first time. Listening to this on audio was initially a bit confusing, but I eventually could tell who was speaking and what timeline they were speaking from. Once that was clear, the story flowed well and I was always eager to return to the story. <br /><br />Maggie O'Farrell knows how to zero in on the labyrinth of emotion we humans carry, and how to help us find each other through those passageways.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer7227502047416513282">3.5 stars (still can't decide if I round up or down)<br/><br/>Many of my friends loved this novel, so I was happy to get my hands on it.<br/><br/>First of all, <i>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</i> wasn't as literary as I expected it to be. <br/>That's not that important, but after reading O'Farrell's memoir, I thought this would belong in the literary fiction category. It's a page-turner and around page eighty, I was invested in the story.<br/><br/>Why was Esme Lennox in an institution for the mentally ill for over sixty years and ho</span>
  <span id="freeText7227502047416513282" style="display:none">3.5 stars (still can't decide if I round up or down)<br /><br />Many of my friends loved this novel, so I was happy to get my hands on it.<br /><br />First of all, <i>The Vanishing Act of Esme Lennox</i> wasn't as literary as I expected it to be. <br />That's not that important, but after reading O'Farrell's memoir, I thought this would belong in the literary fiction category. It's a page-turner and around page eighty, I was invested in the story.<br /><br />Why was Esme Lennox in an institution for the mentally ill for over sixty years and how come nobody in the family ever mentioned her name? These are questions that Iris Lockard is trying to find the answers to as she finds herself the guardian of this unknown woman who turns out to be her great-aunt.<br /><br />Esme Lennox's story is a tragic one and it brings up how easily men, and families, disposed of the women who didn't conform, who were different. I thought to myself, this novel is going to gut me and leave me emotionally deplete. Incredibly enough, it didn't happen. It had many ingredients that made it very readable, some confusing paragraphs, side romances and a brusque, vague ending, which was anti-climatic. <br /><br />While the premise of this novel was excellent, I wasn't as affected by the story, some of the characters weren't fully fleshed out, so in the end, I felt dissatisfied. </span>
